            function createLink(src) {
                var preloadLink = document.createElement("link");
                preloadLink.rel = "preload";
                preloadLink.href = src;
                preloadLink.as = "font";
                preloadLink.type = "font/woff2";
                preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
            }

            var connectionDetail = {
                saveDataEnabled: false,
                slowConnection: false
            };

            var connection = hasConnection();
            if (connection) {
                connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled = connection.saveData;
                if (/\slow-2g|2g/.test(connection.effectiveType)) {
                    connectionDetail.slowConnection = true;
                }
            }

            if (!(connectionDetail.saveDataEnabled || connectionDetail.slowConnection)) {

    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <script type="application/ld+json">{"mainEntity":{"headline":"Sustainability-inspired cell design for a fully recyclable sodium ion battery","description":"Large-scale applications of rechargeable batteries consume nonrenewable resources and produce massive amounts of end-of-life wastes, which raise sustainability concerns in terms of manufacturing, environmental, and ecological costs. Therefore, the recyclability and sustainability of a battery should be considered at the design stage by using naturally abundant resources and recyclable battery technology. Herein, we design a fully recyclable rechargeable sodium ion battery with bipolar electrode structure using Na3V2(PO4)3 as an electrode material and aluminum foil as the shared current collector. Such a design allows exceptional sodium ion battery performance in terms of high-power correspondence and long-term stability and enables the recycling of ∼100% Na3V2(PO4)3 and ∼99.1% elemental aluminum without the release of toxic wastes, resulting in a solid-component recycling efficiency of >98.0%. The successful incorporation of sustainability into battery design suggests that closed-loop recycling and the reutilization of battery materials can be achieved in next-generation energy storage technologies.     <meta name="dc.title" content="Sustainability-inspired cell design for a fully recyclable sodium ion battery">
    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2019 10:1">
    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html">
    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group">
    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-04-29">
    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper">
    <meta name="dc.language" content="En">
    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 This is a U.S. government work and not under copyright protection in the U.S.; foreign copyright protection may apply">
    <meta name="dc.rights" content="2019 This is a U.S. government work and not under copyright protection in the U.S.; foreign copyright protection may apply">
    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com">
    <meta name="dc.description" content="Large-scale applications of rechargeable batteries consume nonrenewable resources and produce massive amounts of end-of-life wastes, which raise sustainability concerns in terms of manufacturing, environmental, and ecological costs. Therefore, the recyclability and sustainability of a battery should be considered at the design stage by using naturally abundant resources and recyclable battery technology. Herein, we design a fully recyclable rechargeable sodium ion battery with bipolar electrode structure using Na3V2(PO4)3 as an electrode material and aluminum foil as the shared current collector. Such a design allows exceptional sodium ion battery performance in terms of high-power correspondence and long-term stability and enables the recycling of ∼100% Na3V2(PO4)3 and ∼99.1% elemental aluminum without the release of toxic wastes, resulting in a solid-component recycling efficiency of &amp;gt;98.0%. The successful incorporation of sustainability into battery design suggests that closed-loop recycling and the reutilization of battery materials can be achieved in next-generation energy storage technologies. Effective recycling technologies represent a solution to the sustainability and environmental consequences of spent rechargeable batteries. Here, the authors show a bipolar electrode design that allows not only good electrochemical performance but a closed loop of material use for sodium ion batteries.">

    <meta name="description" content="Large-scale applications of rechargeable batteries consume nonrenewable resources and produce massive amounts of end-of-life wastes, which raise sustainability concerns in terms of manufacturing, environmental, and ecological costs. Therefore, the recyclability and sustainability of a battery should be considered at the design stage by using naturally abundant resources and recyclable battery technology. Herein, we design a fully recyclable rechargeable sodium ion battery with bipolar electrode structure using Na3V2(PO4)3 as an electrode material and aluminum foil as the shared current collector. Such a design allows exceptional sodium ion battery performance in terms of high-power correspondence and long-term stability and enables the recycling of ∼100% Na3V2(PO4)3 and ∼99.1% elemental aluminum without the release of toxic wastes, resulting in a solid-component recycling efficiency of &amp;gt;98.0%. The successful incorporation of sustainability into battery design suggests that closed-loop recycling and the reutilization of battery materials can be achieved in next-generation energy storage technologies. Effective recycling technologies represent a solution to the sustainability and environmental consequences of spent rechargeable batteries. Here, the authors show a bipolar electrode design that allows not only good electrochemical performance but a closed loop of material use for sodium ion batteries.">

                    <p class="c-article-metrics-bar__details"><a href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/metrics" data-track="click" data-track-action="view metrics" data-track-label="link" rel="nofollow">Metrics <span class="u-visually-hidden">details</span></a></p>
                </li>
            </ul>
        </div>
    
                    
                </header>

                
    

                
    
    

    
    

                
            </div>

        <div class="c-article-body">
            <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Large-scale applications of rechargeable batteries consume nonrenewable resources and produce massive amounts of end-of-life wastes, which raise sustainability concerns in terms of manufacturing, environmental, and ecological costs. Therefore, the recyclability and sustainability of a battery should be considered at the design stage by using naturally abundant resources and recyclable battery technology. Herein, we design a fully recyclable rechargeable sodium ion battery with bipolar electrode structure using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as an electrode material and aluminum foil as the shared current collector. Such a design allows exceptional sodium ion battery performance in terms of high-power correspondence and long-term stability and enables the recycling of <span class="stix">∼</span>100% Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> and <span class="stix">∼</span>99.1% elemental aluminum without the release of toxic wastes, resulting in a solid-component recycling efficiency of &gt;98.0%. The successful incorporation of sustainability into battery design suggests that closed-loop recycling and the reutilization of battery materials can be achieved in next-generation energy storage technologies.</p></div></div></section>

                <div class="main-content">
                    
                        <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Rechargeable batteries have powered extensive types of mobile electronics and shown extraordinary promise for grid storage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dunn, B., Kamath, H. &amp; Tarascon, J.-M. Electrical energy storage for the grid: a battery of choices. Science 334, 928–935 (2011)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e485">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Noorden, R. V. A better battery. Nature 507, 26–28 (2014)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e485_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Obama, B. The irreversible momentum of clean energy. Science 355, 126–129 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e488">3</a></sup>. However, these potential applications call for extremely large-scale battery production and consequently cause growing concerns regarding the massive quantities of end-of-life batteries that seriously threaten environmental and ecological safety<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e492">4</a></sup>. The recycling of end-of-life batteries not only minimizes the demand for critical material resources but also addresses significant concerns regarding environmental pollution and ecological impacts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, J. et al. A review of processes and technologies for the recycling of lithium-ion secondary batteries. J. Power Sources 177, 512–527 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e496">5</a></sup>. Generally, a typical battery is assembled with six components: accessories, electrolyte, separator, cathode, anode, and current collectors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tarascon, J. M. &amp; Armand, M. Issues and challenges facing rechargeable lithium batteries. Nature 414, 359–367 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e500">6</a></sup>. Although accessories, electrolytes, and separators have been efficiently recycled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, J. et al. A review of processes and technologies for the recycling of lithium-ion secondary batteries. J. Power Sources 177, 512–527 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e504">5</a></sup>, it is a substantial challenge to recover the electrode unit containing the current collectors, anode, and cathode, which are the most economically valuable components. The lithium-ion battery (LIB), for example, adopts a unipolar electrode structure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Tarascon, J. M. &amp; Armand, M. Issues and challenges facing rechargeable lithium batteries. Nature 414, 359–367 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e509">6</a></sup>, where cathode materials and anode materials are coated on Al and Cu foils, respectively (shown as one unit in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig1">1a</a>). In the course of large-scale recycling of LIB units, cathode materials, anode materials, and current correctors will inevitably be mixed together, leading to difficult separation where recycling is not economically viable<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e516">4</a></sup>. Hydro- and pyrometallurgical recovery of valuable metals in LIBs has been widely used, providing a possible route for decreasing the demand for mining and enhancing sustainability. These metals can be efficiently recycled, purified, and reused in new battery production. However, there are obvious disadvantages in terms of the high cost and substantial production of nonecologically friendly solid, liquid, and gas wastes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, J. et al. A review of processes and technologies for the recycling of lithium-ion secondary batteries. J. Power Sources 177, 512–527 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e520">5</a></sup>. The potential grid-level energy storage of electricity generated from intermittent renewable sources (e.g., solar and wind) will require vast numbers of LIB cells, thereby creating an even greater drain on rare metal sources (such as lithium) and more extensively impacting the environment when end-of-life LIB cells cannot be effectively recycled.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09933-0/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9933_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09933-0/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9933_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="613"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Design and achievement of bipolar electrode structure. <b>a</b> Schematic of the conventional unipolar electrode structure (one-unit cell). <b>b</b> Schematic of the proposed bipolar electrode structure (two-unit cell). <b>c</b> Linear scanning voltammograms of Al and Cu foils as the cathodes with metallic Na as the anode at 0~5 V at a scanning speed of 0.2 mVs<sup>−1</sup>. <b>d</b> Discharge curves of Al foil as the cathode with metallic Na as the anode at 100 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> for 20 h</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To address these issues, we believe that the recyclability and sustainability of a battery must be considered at the design stage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e562">4</a></sup>. Sodium battery technology could be a promising alternative to LIBs for grid-level energy storage due to the widely established competitive energy and power densities, low cost, and environmental benignity of sodium batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dunn, B., Kamath, H. &amp; Tarascon, J.-M. Electrical energy storage for the grid: a battery of choices. Science 334, 928–935 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e566">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Thackeray, M. M., Wolverton, C. &amp; Isaacs, E. D. Electrical energy storage for transportation—approaching the limits of, and going beyond, lithium-ion batteries. Energy Environ. Sci. 5, 7854–7863 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e569">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kim, S.-W., Seo, D.-H., Ma, X., Ceder, G. &amp; Kang, K. Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 2, 710–721 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e572">8</a></sup>. One good example is high-temperature sodium–sulfur batteries (HT-NSBs), which were successfully commercialized in the 1980s. Furthermore, sodium salts are highly available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Hwang, J. Y., Myung, S. T. &amp; Sun, Y. K. Sodium-ion batteries: present and future. Chem. Soc. Rev. 46, 3529–3614 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e576">9</a></sup>. Recently, room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) have been considered a safer energy storage technology than HT-NSBs. However, NIBs have a similar electrode structure to that of LIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Kim, S.-W., Seo, D.-H., Ma, X., Ceder, G. &amp; Kang, K. Electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries: potential alternatives to current lithium-ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 2, 710–721 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e580">8</a></sup> and thereby experiences the same recovery issues when applied to grid-level energy storage. The historical crisis of white plastics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ren, X. Biodegradable plastics: a solution or a challenge? J. Clean. Prod. 11, 27–40 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e584">10</a></sup> should teach us that prescient product design could help increase recycling and reduce environmental impact.</p><p>Here, to achieve viable recyclability and sustainability of NIBs, we design a bipolar electrode structure instead of a unipolar electrode structure: the cathode and anode materials are coated on both sides of a shared metallic current collector (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig1">1b</a>). Al foil and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) are selected for the shared current collector and active materials of the cathode and anode, respectively. Al foil enables the bipolar electrode structure to benefit from the lack of alloying reaction between Na and Al. The electrode materials are readily collected from waste NIBs via a basic treatment. Consequently, over 98.0% of the solid components of waste NIBs are recycled without the release of toxic wastes. In particular, the high-value NVP is separated and collected with a recycling efficiency of 100%. Recycled NVP simply treated by a direct regeneration process is reused in a new NIB and exhibits excellent electrochemical performance. Therefore, the adoption of such a bipolar electrode structure for NIBs confers the NVP material with closed-loop sustainability.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">A fully recyclable sodium ion battery design</h3><p>In traditional NIBs, Al and Cu foils are used as current collectors for the cathode and anode, respectively. Their electrochemical applicability as a universal current collector was investigated in the full voltage window of 0–5 V, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig1">1c</a>. Al foil shows a negligible current from 0 to 5 V with high stability, while Cu foil exhibits a sharp rise in current from ca. 3.6 V due to the oxidation of Cu at high potentials<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Gao, T. et al. Li3V2(PO4)3 as a cathode additive for the over-discharge protection of lithium ion batteries. RSC Adv. 6, 76933–76937 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e617">11</a></sup>. The discharge platform of Al foil is under 0 V, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig1">1d</a>, demonstrating that there is no alloying reaction between Al foil and metallic Na. This is further confirmed by the existence of metallic Na in ex situ XRD (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Therefore, we designed an NIB with a bipolar electrode structure using Al foil as a single current collector, simplifying the electrode structure to achieve more efficient recycling and saving costs by eliminating the need for a Cu foil current collector.</p><p>After designing the bipolar electrode structure and confirming the selection of a single Al foil current collector, we examined active materials by establishing a system among the three with water to form NaOH (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ1">1</a>) and then corrode the Al foil (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ2">2</a>). Elemental Al can be collected in the form of Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> via acid-base neutralization with a suitable amount of HCl (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ3">3</a>). The final waste is mainly NaCl solution. In this work, we chose NVP as the cathode material due to its high rate performance, long lifespan, and abundant reserves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, T. et al. All-climate sodium ion batteries based on the NASICON electrode materials. Nano Energy 30, 756–761 (2016)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e641">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Saravanan, K., Mason, C. W., Rudola, A., Wong, K. H. &amp; Balaya, P. The first report on excellent cycling stability and superior rate capability of Na3V2(PO4)3 for sodium ion batteries. Adv. Energy Mater. 3, 444–450 (2013)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e641_1">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kundu, D., Adams, B. D., Duffort, V., Vajargah, S. H. &amp; Nazar, L. F. A high-capacity and long-life aqueous rechargeable zinc battery using a metal oxide intercalation cathode. Nat. Energy 1, 16119–16126 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e644">14</a></sup>. Highly toxic elemental V needs to be recycled<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Moskalyk, R. R. &amp; Alfantazi, A. M. Processing of vanadium: a review. Miner. Eng. 16, 793–805 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e649">15</a></sup>. Consequently, the combination of serial chemical reactions (i.e., Eqs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ1">1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ3">3</a>) among the cathode, anode, and current collector not only facilitates the rapid separation of individual cell components but also enables the effective recycling of specific materials. To recycle the electrolyte and stainless steel accessories, NIBs can share the recycling technologies of LIBs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Xu, J. et al. A review of processes and technologies for the recycling of lithium-ion secondary batteries. J. Power Sources 177, 512–527 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e659">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Liu, Y., Mu, D., Zheng, R. &amp; Dai, C. Supercritical CO2 extraction of organic carbonate-based electrolytes of lithium-ion batteries. RSC Adv. 4, 54525–54531 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e662">16</a></sup>.</p><div id="Equ1" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Na}}({\mathrm{s}}) + {\mathrm{H}}_2{\mathrm{O}}({\mathrm{l}}) \to {\mathrm{NaOH}}({\mathrm{aq}}) + {\mathrm{H}}_2({\mathrm{g}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (1)
                </div></div><div id="Equ2" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{NaOH}}({\mathrm{aq}}) + {\mathrm{Al}}({\mathrm{s}}) \to {\mathrm{NaAlO}}_2({\mathrm{aq}}) + {\mathrm{H}}_2({\mathrm{g}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (2)
                </div></div><div id="Equ3" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{NaAlO}}_2({\mathrm{aq}}) + {\mathrm{HCl}}({\mathrm{aq}}) \to {\mathrm{NaCl}}({\mathrm{aq}}) + {\mathrm{Al}}\left( {{\mathrm{OH}}} \right)_3({\mathrm{s}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (3)
                </div></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">A high-performance and stable sodium ion battery</h3><p>Based on the above design, we assembled an asymmetric NIB cell with two units (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">2</a>). The cell consists of six components: (i) carbon-coated NVP (NVP@C) cathode (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">3</a>); (ii) metallic Na anode; (iii) Al foil current collector; (iv) 1M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethylene carbonate (DEC) as the electrolyte; (v) glass fiber separator; and (vi) accessories including stainless steel cases and seal rings. Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig2">2a</a> shows the initial charge and discharge curves of a two-unit NIB cell. Benefitting from the voltage contribution of each unit (~3.4 V per unit), the NIB cell readily exhibits high voltage platforms of ca. 6.9 V and 6.5 V at the charge and discharge states, respectively. Stable cycling of over 100 times demonstrates the effectiveness of the bipolar electrode structure (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig2">2b</a>). Further illustrating the high-rate features of the bipolar electrode structure, the NIB cell exhibits remarkable rate performance, i.e., up to 20 C, with a high discharge voltage (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig2">2c</a>). The bipolar NIB cells yield an energy density of ca. 368.2 Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (normalized to NVP@C, i.e., 92.1 Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> when normalized to the full cell) at 1 <i>C</i> and a power density of ca. 7.3 kW&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (normalized to NVP@C, i.e., 1.83 kW&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> when normalized to the full cell) at 20 C<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gratz, E., Sa, Q., Apelian, D. &amp; Wang, Y. A closed loop process for recycling spent lithium ion batteries. J. Power Sources 262, 255–262 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1051">17</a></sup>, which make them potentially applicable in grid-level energy storage. Peak regulation in grid-level energy storage for intermittent renewable sources—which require corresponding high-power batteries—is a typical application<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dunn, B., Kamath, H. &amp; Tarascon, J.-M. Electrical energy storage for the grid: a battery of choices. Science 334, 928–935 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1055">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Thackeray, M. M., Wolverton, C. &amp; Isaacs, E. D. Electrical energy storage for transportation—approaching the limits of, and going beyond, lithium-ion batteries. Energy Environ. Sci. 5, 7854–7863 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1058">7</a></sup>. To simulate this application, pulse current charge/discharge tests were performed. At a rate of 20 C for 30 s, the voltage platforms during charge/discharge are relatively steady within the pulse period (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">4</a>). The large cross-sectional area between the cathode and anode enables low and homogeneous current density, resulting in small voltage drops even at high rates. The NIB cell repeated cycling over 2000 times (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig2">2d</a>), illustrating its remarkable stability.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09933-0/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9933_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09933-0/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9933_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="345"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Electrochemical behavior of a two-unit bipolar NIB cell. <b>a</b> Initial charge and discharge curves at 1 C in a voltage range from 5.7 to 7.6 V. <b>b</b> Cycling 100 times at 1 C. <b>c</b> Rate capabilities from 1 to 20 C. <b>d</b> The voltages of long cycles at the beginning and the end of each step under fast charge/discharge</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We can further enlarge the energy and power densities of the NIB by assembling battery packs from two to three (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">5a</a>) and five (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">6a</a>) units. The output voltage of the cells linearly increases with the number of units. For three units, the NIB cell delivers a discharge voltage of ca. 9.8 V at 10 C with high power and excellent cycling (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">5b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">5c</a>). The discharge voltage is increased to 16.4 V for five units with long-term cyclability (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">6b and c</a>). The high performance is attributed to the proposed NIB design combining a bipolar electrode structure with a high-rate electrode material.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Fully rejuvenated electrochemical performance of the sodium ion battery</h3><p>The most important advantage of our designed NIB with a bipolar electrode structure is the recyclability of the electrode materials in the battery unit, i.e., NVP and elemental Al. After cycling, we disassembled a two-unit NIB cell to investigate the feasibility of the proposed recyclability. (Supplementary Movie&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM4">1</a> shows detailed operations and phenomena in the recycling process). The NIB cell was first predischarged and subsequently disassembled into fractions. The electrolyte was difficult to recycle due to the small amount of organic electrolyte used in coin-type NIB cells. In the scope of this work, we focus on solid-component recycling in spent NIBs with a bipolar electrode structure. All fractions were immersed in a recycling tank filled with water at room temperature. The NVP@C slurry was naturally detached from the Al foil (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">7</a>), and hydrogen was the byproduct (Eqs.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ2">2</a>). After filtration, we easily recovered the stainless steel cases, sealing rings, and glass fibers; the cathode slurry containing NVP@C, the conductive additive, and the polymeric binder was also collected.</p><p>An additional washing step for the recycled NVP slurry was performed, leading to a 20% increase in wastewater. This wastewater from additional washing steps can be reused in recycling tanks. The residual contamination was tested by ICP. The vanadium and phosphate concentrations were less than 2 ppb in the leachate, indicating that the NVP@C is very stable in basic solution. In addition, no Al residual was detected in the recycled NVP sample. Subsequently, according to the TGA curve of NVP@C, the carbon coating on the surface of the NVP particles was burned at 500 °C, while NVP was oxidized by V from +3 to +5 as the temperature increased (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">8</a>). Therefore, the obtained cathode slurry was sintered in air at 550 °C to recycle NVP. Appropriate amounts of diluted HCl solution were used to collect elemental Al in the form of Al(OH)<sub>3</sub>, with mainly NaCl aqueous solution left (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ3">3</a>).</p><p>Weight breakdowns of the NIB cell in the initial and recycled states are listed in Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">1</a> and Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">9</a>. In addition to achieving almost 100% recycling of accessories and separators, we successfully recycled almost 100% of the NVP from the cathode slurry and 99.1% of the elemental Al (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">10</a> and Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">1</a>). Compared with hydro- and pyrometallurgical recovery of valuable metals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Winslow, K. M., Laux, S. J. &amp; Townsend, T. G. A review on the growing concern and potential management strategies of waste lithium-ion batteries. Resour. Conserv. Recy. 129, 263–277 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1162">18</a></sup>, our process for solid-component recycling is cost-saving, highly efficient, and environmentally friendly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1167">4</a></sup>. The whole solid-component recycling efficiency of our proposed NIB cell is ca. 98.1%, which is close to that of lead-acid batteries with heavily regulated disposal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1171">4</a></sup>. The only losses are the metallic Na, conductive additive, and polymer binder (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><p>More importantly, this design can also be extended to other battery systems. As an example, we also assembled a symmetric NIB unit (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">12a</a>) where NVP acts as the active material in both the anode and cathode in a symmetric configuration (Supplementary Note&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">2</a>). The symmetric NIB cell also exhibits stable electrochemical performance (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">12b</a>). Because both the cathode and anode use identical NVP@C material, diluted NaOH solution instead of water was used to separate the NVP and recycle elemental Al in the recycling process. The recovery for this symmetric NIB cell (Supplementary Movie&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM5">2</a>) is therefore as convenient as that for the asymmetric NIB cell mentioned above. A solid-component recycling efficiency of ca. 99.7% was achieved with this system (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">13</a> and Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">3</a>).</p><p>The cathode is the most expensive part of a battery cell and, to a great extent, determines the cost of the cell<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gratz, E., Sa, Q., Apelian, D. &amp; Wang, Y. A closed loop process for recycling spent lithium ion batteries. J. Power Sources 262, 255–262 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1204">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Vaalma, C., Buchholz, D., Weil, M. &amp; Passerini, S. A cost and resource analysis of sodium-ion batteries. Nat. Rev. Mater. 3, 18013 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1207">19</a></sup>. After recycling, we continued to demonstrate the sustainability of recycled NVP in a closed loop, as shown in Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig3">3</a>. The most efficient and simple recovery method is to rejuvenate the cathode, which is called direct recovery<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1214">4</a></sup>. In other words, cathode materials can be recycled and reused in the new battery design with a simple and low-cost method rather than conventional hydro- or pyrometallurgic processes, significantly saving the environmental and material costs of the fundamental elements of the cathode. We first reprocessed the recycled NVP for a new battery unit by direct regeneration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, X., Zhang, J., Song, D., Song, J. &amp; Zhang, L. Direct regeneration of recycled cathode material mixture from scrapped LiFePO4 batteries. J. Power Sources 345, 78–84 (2017)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1218">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Shi, Y., Chen, G. &amp; Chen, Z. Effective regeneration of LiCoO2 from spent lithium-ion batteries: a direct approach towards high-performance active particles. Green. Chem. 20, 851–862 (2018)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1218_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Shi, Y., Chen, G., Liu, F., Yue, X. &amp; Chen, Z. Resolving the compositional and structural defects of degraded LiNixCoyMnzO2 particles to directly regenerate high-performance lithium-ion battery cathodes. ACS Energy Lett. 3, 1683–1692 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1221">22</a></sup>. The recycled NVP was calcined at 800 °C in an inert atmosphere with appropriate amounts of sucrose to form reprocessed NVP@C. The XRD patterns (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig4">4a</a>) are well indexed to a high-crystallinity NASICON structure (JCPDS No. 62-0345) without impurity, indicating there is no decomposition of the recycled NVP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, T. et al. All-climate sodium ion batteries based on the NASICON electrode materials. Nano Energy 30, 756–761 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1229">12</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" (blue), reformation of NVP@C from the recycled NVP; Loop 2 (purple), recycling of elemental Al in the form of Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> and elemental Na in the form of NaCl through the processes of reaction, regeneration, and reprocessing</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09933-0/MediaObjects/41467_2019_9933_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" original NVP@C. <b>a</b> XRD spectra. <b>b</b> Charge/discharge curves at 5 C. <b>c</b> Cyclic voltammetry curves</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-eds-i-chevron-right-small"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">14a</a>) demonstrates that the morphology (including particle size and porosity) of reprocessed NVP@C is similar to that of the original NVP@C (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">14b</a>). The electrochemical behavior (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig4">4b</a>) and cyclic voltammetry measurement (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig4">4c</a>) of reprocessed NVP@C are similar to those of the original NVP@C, demonstrating that the reprocessed NVP@C has good electrochemical reversibility. The reprocessed NVP@C material is also capable of fast charging and discharging (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">15a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">15b</a>). The long cycling performance of 500 cycles further confirms the excellent capacity retention of reprocessed NVP@C (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">15c</a>). Furthermore, the NVP recycled from the above-tested NIB cells was repeatedly implemented. The reprocessed NVP still exhibited very good performance with stable long-term cycling in NIBs (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">16</a>). All of the above results demonstrate the closed-loop utilization of the NVP material for a sustainable NIB with a bipolar electrode structure.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>We attribute the above-demonstrated exceptional rate performance and full recycling operation of NIBs to the unique design of their bipolar electrode architecture (Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig1">1b</a>). First, a bipolar electrode structure naturally accomplishes an internal series sequence without additional connecting accessories, allowing high current density transfer between the cathode and anode. Second, Al foil as a universal current collector for both the cathode and anode in the NIB enables the achievement of the proposed bipolar electrode structure and simplifies the NIB components. Third, the basic chemicals help establish an association among Al, metallic sodium and water, not only facilitating the separation of the active material (i.e., NVP) from the current collector but also enabling the effective recycling of Al, producing mainly NaCl aqueous solution as waste. Fourth, the NVP is directly regenerated without complex synthetic processing, and the bipolar electrode structure enables the rechargeable battery to use a low amount of steel and Al, which favors decreased energy consumption in the recycling process. Finally, Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Fig4">4</a> shows the fully rejuvenated electrochemical performance of the NVP in NIBs, demonstrating our proposed battery concept of performance, recyclability, and reuse, which critically depends on the initial stage of battery design. Although NVP reprocessing was only performed twice due to time constraints, we believe that recycled NVP can be further processed many times, and the current work has demonstrated the feasibility of closed-loop recycling of NVP.</p><p>Further explanation is needed here to claim that we have developed a fully recyclable NIB. First, the electrolyte can be recovered by sharing the LIB technologies. In general, electrolyte salts are dissolved in organic solvents (e.g., propylene carbonate and ethylene carbonate) with functional additives (e.g., vinyl carbonate and biphenyl). Currently, the main electrolyte recycling methods are classified as vacuum pyrolysis, organic solvent extraction, and supercritical CO<sub>2</sub> extraction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Sun, L. &amp; Qiu, K. Vacuum pyrolysis and hydrometallurgical process for the recovery of valuable metals from spent lithium-ion batteries. J. Hazard Mater. 194, 378–384 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1328">23</a></sup>. Referring to a previous work by Liu et al., high-efficiency recycling of the electrolyte has been demonstrated, and the recycled electrolyte can be reused in new batteries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Liu, Y., Mu, D., Zheng, R. &amp; Dai, C. Supercritical CO2 extraction of organic carbonate-based electrolytes of lithium-ion batteries. RSC Adv. 4, 54525–54531 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1332">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Liu, Y. et al. Purification and characterization of reclaimed electrolytes from spent lithium-ion batteries. J. Phys. Chem. C. 121, 4181–4187 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1335">24</a></sup>. Second, the recycling of metallic anodes is also highly feasible. Currently, metallic Na and Al are mainly produced by the electrolysis of molten salts at high temperature. We can expect that metallic Na and Al can be extracted&nbsp; well by melting recycled NaCl and Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> at 600 and 950 °C through an electrolysis process<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Polmear, I., StJohn, D., Nie, J.-F. &amp; Qian, M. in Light Alloys (Fifth Edition) 1–29 (Elsevier, Amsterdam, 2017)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1341">25</a></sup>&nbsp;for further usage, respectively. Although these recycling and reprocessing steps for the electrolyte and metallic Na/Al sources were not performed in this work, we think the above-verified knowledge enables a fully recyclable NIB.</p><p>In addition, safety is the first priority during practical recycling processes and emission treatment. On the one hand, hydrogen as a byproduct may pose safety concerns in recycling processes. Therefore, special treatment must be performed prior to battery recycling. For waste NIBs using metallic Na, the remaining metallic Na is highly active. The waste NIB is first cut into small pieces. The resultant slags should be placed in a dry ventilated place. The Na can react with O<sub>2</sub> to form Na<sub>2</sub>O (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ4">4</a>), and subsequently, some of the Na<sub>2</sub>O can react with CO<sub>2</sub> further to form Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ5">5</a>). If this operation is executed well, the amount of byproduct hydrogen can be significantly reduced with the addition of H<sub>2</sub>O into the NIB waste (Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ6">6</a>) compared with the amount obtained from direct water treatment. Moreover, the Al foil can be collected prior to full dissolution. We found that the surface of Al foil is readily corroded by alkali solution, leading to rapid separation of the electrode slurry and Al foil (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">7</a>). Therefore, the fast recycling of Al foil delaminated from the electrode slurry can reduce the amount of hydrogen in the recycling process. Notably, ventilation must be carried out from the beginning to the end of the battery recycling process. On the other hand, the waste gas should be considered with regard to safety issues involving the environment and humans. The fluoridated binder (PVDF) is first directly burned to remove the binder from the recycled NVP@C slurry. However, this process can produce volatile organic compounds (VOCs), which are highly toxic to the environment and humans. The off-gas must be treated further at higher temperature to enable the efficient decontamination of VOCs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Dueso, N. Volatile Organic Compounds Treatment Techniques.&nbsp;Studies in Environ. Sci. 61, 263−276 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1376">26</a></sup>.</p><div id="Equ4" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Na}}({\mathrm{s}}) + {\mathrm{O}}_2({\mathrm{g}}) \to {\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{O}}({\mathrm{s}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (4)
                </div></div><div id="Equ5" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{O}}({\mathrm{s}}) + {\mathrm{CO}}_2({\mathrm{s}}) \to {\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{CO}}_3\left( {\mathrm{s}} \right)$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (5)
                </div></div><div id="Equ6" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{O}}({\mathrm{s}}) + {\mathrm{H}}_2{\mathrm{O}}({\mathrm{l}}) \to {\mathrm{NaOH}}({\mathrm{aq}})$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (6)
                </div></div><p>In summary, our proposed bipolar electrode configuration for NIBs achieves high rate performance and full recycling operation. By taking advantage of this electrode design, we significantly diminish battery cost with reduced consumption of energy and material resources, making the NIBs environmentally sustainable for grid-level energy storage, in contrast to several traditional recycling methods (Supplementary Table&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">2</a>). This design presents a possible solution to the most recent concerns regarding abandoned batteries with low recycling efficiency and high environmental risk<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Gies, E. Lazarus batteries. Nature 526, S100–S101 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1698">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Evarts, E. C. To the limits of lithium. Nature 526, 593–595 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1701">27</a></sup>. In the future, some features are a must for NIBs used in large-scale energy storage systems: (1) A high recycling rate, enabling a fast response to energy fluctuations in renewable energy and peak regulation in smart grids. (2) A low battery cost, making batteries more economical by reducing the consumption of energy and material resources. (3) Excellent environmental sustainability, diminishing all the traditional environmental risks from battery production, abandoned batteries, battery recycling and so on. We believe that a comprehensive consideration of recyclability and sustainability in battery design and material selection could make next-generation energy storage systems highly efficient and fully green.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Synthesis of NVP@C composite</h3><p>The synthesis of carbon-coated Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP@C) composite was detailed in our previous work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Liu, T. et al. All-climate sodium ion batteries based on the NASICON electrode materials. Nano Energy 30, 756–761 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1725">12</a></sup>. Briefly, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (AR), NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> (AR), Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (AR) and sucrose were weighed in a molar ratio of Na:V:P:C = 3:2:3:4. All raw materials were mixed, dispersed in 30 ml alcohol and ball-milled for 6 h in a planetary mill at 400RPM. The as-obtained mixture was dried at 80 °C in an oven to evaporate the alcohol. The remaining precursor was transferred into a porcelain boat and calcined at 300 °C for 2 h to obtain the bulk intermediate, which was ball-milled again for 6 h in alcohol and dried overnight. The resultant nanosized intermediate was sintered at 800 °C for 8 h under an Ar atmosphere in a tube furnace and cooled to room temperature to yield NVP@C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Material characterization</h3><p>The morphology and structure of the samples were investigated by a field-emission scanning electron microscope (SEM) on SU8010. X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were recorded on a Rigaku D/MAX-2550-PC X-ray diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 0.154 nm). The 2<i>θ</i> degree range used in the measurements was from 20° to 80°. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed on a Pyris 1 TGA (Perkin Elmer) system under air flow (50–800 °C, 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>). Inductive coupled plasma (ICP) emission spectrometry was executed on an iCAP6300 (detection limit: 2–200 µgL<sup>−1</sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Assembly of NIB cells</h3><p>NVP@C (80 wt. %), acetylene black (AB, 10 wt. %), and polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF, Aldrich) binder (10 wt. %) were dispersed in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, Aldrich) to form a homogeneous slurry. The obtained slurry was spread on an Al foil and dried at 100 °C overnight in a vacuum oven to remove the NMP solvent. The working electrode was round in shape with a diameter of 12 mm. The average loading mass of the NVP@C electrode was 2–5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The cell was assembled based on an asymmetric configuration, meaning that the cathode and anode used different active materials. An asymmetric bipolar NIB cell was fabricated with an NVP@C electrode, metallic Na disk, glass fiber (GA-55) separator, sealing ring, and organic electrolyte in an Ar-filled glove box. NaClO<sub>4</sub> (1 M) in a 1:1 (v/v) mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (DEC) was used as the electrolyte, which was completely sealed by the Al current collector and sealing ring in each independent cell. In addition, the cells were assembled based on a symmetric configuration for comparison, meaning that the cathode and anode used the same active material. A symmetric bipolar NIB cell was assembled with an NVP@C cathode, NVP@C anode, glass fiber (GA-55) separator, sealing ring, and organic electrolyte in an Ar-filled glove box. The organic electrolyte and sealing conditions were the same as those for the asymmetric bipolar NIB cell mentioned above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Electrochemical test</h3><p>For the NIB cells with bipolar electrode structure, galvanostatic charge-discharge tests were conducted using a custom-made LANDCT battery tester at different rates. Fast charge and discharge tests, which simulate an electrochemical battery system with high-power input/output to stabilize grid fluctuations arising from intermittent wind or solar energy supplies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Dunn, B., Kamath, H. &amp; Tarascon, J.-M. Electrical energy storage for the grid: a battery of choices. Science 334, 928–935 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1780">1</a></sup>, were performed over different times and current densities in a Solartron Analytical Cell Test System (1470E multichannel potentiostats). During the fast charge and discharge tests, the initial voltage of fast charging, end voltage of fast charging, initial voltage of fast discharging, and end voltage of fast discharging were recorded in each cycle (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">5</a>). For recycled NVP@C–Na cells, galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were investigated using LANDCT 2001A battery testers at different rates with a voltage window of 2.5–4.3 V. Cyclic voltammetry measurements (2.5–4.3 V, 0.1 mVs<sup>−1</sup>) were performed on a CHI 660E electrochemical workstation. For the characterization of Al and Cu foils, linear sweep voltammetry (LSV, 0.01–5.0 V, 0.2 mVs<sup>−1</sup>) on a CHI 604E electrochemical workstation was used. For NVP@C–NVP@C cells, galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were conducted using LANDCT 2001A battery testers at different rates with a voltage window of 2–4.4 V. All the voltages are referenced to Na/Na<sup>+</sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Reaction, regeneration, and reprocessing procedure</h3><p>The NIB cell containing the metallic Na anode was disassembled and soaked in water for 1 h. During this period, the Al foil current collector was dissolved in the NaOH solution formed from the reaction between metallic Na and water. After filtering the solution, the stainless steel cases, sealing rings, and separator were collected. The NVP@C slurry was also collected and dried overnight (Supplementary Fig.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#MOESM1">8</a>). The recycled NVP@C slurry was sintered at 550 °C for 4 h in air to remove the coating carbon, conductive additive, and polymer binder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Pandey, M., Joshi, G. M., Mukherjee, A. &amp; Thomas, P. Electrical properties and thermal degradation of poly(vinyl chloride)/polyvinylidene fluoride/ZnO polymer nanocomposites. Polym. Int. 65, 1098–1106 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1805">28</a></sup> and then cooled to room temperature to produce NVP powder. The recycled NVP powder was mixed with sucrose and further sintered at 800 °C for 6 h in an inert atmosphere to obtain reprocessed NVP@C. Sucrose was weighed at a molar ratio of V:C = 1:2. For the NIB cell using NVP@C as the anode, the recycling process was the same except that diluted NaOH solution was used instead of water. The Al was recycled using an appropriate amount of diluted HCl solution to deposit white Al(OH)<sub>3.</sub> The pH value of the solution was tuned to ca. 5.37. The recycling, reprocessing, and reuse of the NVP@C materials in bipolar NIBs was performed two times. Each time, all NVP@C was reused in NIBs after regeneration.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Calculation of energy and power densities</h3><p>The energy density of NVP was calculated according to Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ7">7</a>:</p><div id="Equ7" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${{W}} = {{U}} \times {{Q}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (7)
                </div></div><p>where <i>W</i> represents the specific energy density of NVP, <i>U</i> represents the terminal voltage of one cell, and <i>Q</i> represents the specific capacity of NVP at different rates. The power density of NVP was calculated according to Eq.&nbsp;<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="equation anchor" href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#Equ8">8</a>:</p><div id="Equ8" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${{P}} = {{U}} \times {{I}}$$</span></div><div class="c-article-equation__number">
                    (8)
                </div></div><p>where <i>P</i> represents the specific energy density of NVP, <i>U</i> represents the terminal voltage of one cell, and <i>I</i> represents the overall current of NVP. The energy and power densities of the cell were calculated by referring to the weight percent of cathode material<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Gratz, E., Sa, Q., Apelian, D. &amp; Wang, Y. A closed loop process for recycling spent lithium ion batteries. J. Power Sources 262, 255–262 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1902">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Georgi-Maschler, T., Friedrich, B., Weyhe, R., Heegn, H. &amp; Rutz, M. Development of a recycling process for Li-ion batteries. J. Power Sources 207, 173–182 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-019-09933-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d5232902e1905">29</a></sup>, which accounts for one quarter of the weight of a full NIB. Therefore, one full cell theoretically has 25% energy and power densities based on NVP.</p></div></div></section>
                    
                </div>

              <p>The data that support the findings of this study are available from the corresponding authors upon reasonable request.</p>
